      $('_tip_680').observe('prototip:shown', function() {
        if (this.up('#box')) {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/27653394?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 18, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/446464-kathryn" class="user" title="Kathryn">Kathryn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/89369.The_Te_of_Piglet" title="Paperback 9781405204279" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">don't bother; read Tao of Pooh instead!</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/446464-kathryn?shelf=stalled" class="actionLinkLite">stalled</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer27653394">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3790613109827918750">I only made it about half-way through this book and that was a struggle--I continued only because I had such fond memories of "The Tao of Pooh." In my opinion, this is not a worthy sequel. All the sweetness and warmth of the first book, the Mile-esque style of writing, is gone. So, too, is much discussion of Taoism--and even of Piglet, for that matter. Rather, it seems a soapbox for the author's disillusionment/disgust with Western culture. Would have been much more effective if he kept the focu</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3790613109827918750">I only made it about half-way through this book and that was a struggle--I continued only because I had such fond memories of "The Tao of Pooh." In my opinion, this is not a worthy sequel. All the sweetness and warmth of the first book, the Mile-esque style of writing, is gone. So, too, is much discussion of Taoism--and even of Piglet, for that matter. Rather, it seems a soapbox for the author's disillusionment/disgust with Western culture. Would have been much more effective if he kept the focus on Taoism's good points, rather than using so much ink slamming the West.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_100586429">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100586429" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3633038-jesse-field" class="left imgcol" title="Jesse Field"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1273174024p2/3633038.jpg" alt="Jesse Field" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/100586429?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 01, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3633038-jesse-field" class="user" title="Jesse Field">Jesse Field</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/89369.The_Te_of_Piglet" title="Paperback 9781405204279" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3633038-jesse-field?shelf=essay" class="actionLinkLite">essay</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3633038-jesse-field?shelf=american" class="actionLinkLite">american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer100586429">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer299019074812148965">The Fèn of Eeyore.<br /><br />One day I was walking through the bog, gazing wistfully at the muddy water, when I came across Eeyore in his den. A lovely smell was coming out of the crumbling donkey shelter.<br /><br />"Hallo old friend. What's cooking?"<br /><br />"Hola, amigo. Why this is just a simple zuppa verde. Thistles and nettles from the bog you know, but quite good when cooked in buttered broth. Farina grains add bulk and thickness, what what. Ho ho! But what's this, you're looking a bit doom-and-gloom, my friend. What ha</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText299019074812148965">The Fèn of Eeyore.<br /><br />One day I was walking through the bog, gazing wistfully at the muddy water, when I came across Eeyore in his den. A lovely smell was coming out of the crumbling donkey shelter.<br /><br />"Hallo old friend. What's cooking?"<br /><br />"Hola, amigo. Why this is just a simple zuppa verde. Thistles and nettles from the bog you know, but quite good when cooked in buttered broth. Farina grains add bulk and thickness, what what. Ho ho! But what's this, you're looking a bit doom-and-gloom, my friend. What has happened to your cheerful demeanor?"<br /><br />"Oh Eeyore, it is Very Good to see you again. I've just been reading <em>The Te of Piglet</em> you see, and it has got me feeling...that is, a bit..."<br /><br />"Ho-ho! Say no more. That book has the wrong title, to start with. Actually it has the wrong everything. It's not really about anything. Though of course certain parties are trodden on..."<br /><br />"Yes, I believe that's what's left me so conflicted. And, well, so you know, then, that...uhm..."<br /><br />"Yes, I know. Oh I know. It was <em>me</em> what was trod, yup? <em>I'm</em> the odd bits which got smithered on. I certainly know all this. You needn't mention it. And after all, one can't complain. I have my friends. Somebody spoke to me only yesterday. And was it last week or the week before that Rabbit bumped into me and said 'Bother!'. The Social Round. Always something going on."<br /><br />"You know, Eeyore, I can't help but feel that this time this Tao and Te fellow has gone over the line. I mean, really, Educational System Eeyores? And what's this nonsense about 'Eeyore Amazons'? Could you make head or tail out of that (so to speak)?"<br /><br />"Ah yes, those misfit feminists with their 'overabundance of masculine energies.' Do you mean to say you find the fellow unqualified to measure the proper amount of 'masculine energy'?"<br /><br />"The thought had occurred to me."<br /><br />"Haha, well it's all quite amusing really. In a quiet way, that is, and without being really helpful. As you no doubt gathered, the book is said to be about Piglet, but there's really very little of Piglet in it at all."<br /><br />"Quite true -- I was surprised at that."<br /><br />"You shouldn't be, my friend! Oh, I tell you, in this world nearly every creature suffers from a Positively Startling Lack of Brain. The result of course is that the books they write aren't about what they thought they would write about. Probably this book did start out about Piglet, but soon a bit of chaff flew by, or else a new smell entered the air, and before the author knew it, he was Whining again, as he is of course wont to do.<br /><br />Now for your information, in China, Whining is actually an art."<br /><br />"Really? But isn't that annoying to readers?"<br /><br />"Not at all! Not when done properly. And not if the listeners feel the same way about matters. Why, down in the bogs and swamps of China I have visited with Donkeys, Monkeys, DonkMonks, and Key-Dons of all shapes and sizes, all perfectly able to spend an entire evening Whining. Whining's good for the soul, after all, for once you're done you've cleaned yourself out. You've taken out all your bile and whatnot and spillt it out into the bog water, where it makes no difference at all."<br /><br />"Oh! I think I see where you are going with this. And so The Te of Piglet..."<br /><br />"Right. It's entirely whining, from beginning to end. What little we see of poor Piglet is purposely obscured to make it seem as if we are all actually already either Whiners or Worth Whining About. Which is perfectly true, by the way!"<br /><br />"I see. So back to the title. You said it had the wrong title. Perhaps it should be called Whining?"<br /><br />"Yes. Since our friend likes Chinese terms so much, he might have called it after the Chinese term for a "whining," which refers to the scattering and drawing out of the bile, or indignation, which is called "fen." "Whining" is a verb, but "fen" is a nice solid noun which refers to the bile in the heart. So I do think he should have called it The Fen."<br /><br />"I see, yes. But then again it wouldn't do to call it The Fen. It needs an Animal, don't you think? Perhaps The Fen of Piglet would be better?"<br /><br />"Don't be daft. That silly bit of fluff hasn't the confidence to Whine in a full-throated, belly-emptying way. No, no, Piglet would never do."<br /><br />"Come to think of it, as we said before, the Animal that whines most is you, Eeyore."<br /><br />"Why thank you very much. I was so hoping someone would notice!"<br /><br />"Ah! Now I have it. Our friend only <em>seems</em> to dislike you, Eeyore. He whines about Eeyores. But since Eeyores are the whiners, then he too is an Eeyore. Why don't we call the book The Fen of Eeyore?"<br /><br />"Oh, my goodness. I couldn't accept that. Oh, no, really. Me? The star of a book? Well, suppose I do know a thing or two. I've a brain. Say, do you want to stay awhile? Have some <em>zuppa</em>?"<br /><br />"Wonderful! I've some beer in this bag, if that interests you."<br /><br />"It may interest me, sir. Perhaps as much as anything ever does, in any case. Drinking beer never did anyone any good. Then again, the world is in such a state, a few beers are certainly called for. Oh! Everybody has been Wrong about Everything. I tell you! Especially Wrong about Me. Why, what Whining we shall have tonight, sir..."<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="299019074812148965">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1138604373">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1138604373" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/27386523-robert" class="left imgcol" title="Robert"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1448995493p2/27386523.jpg" alt="Robert" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1138604373?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 18, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/27386523-robert" class="user" title="Robert">Robert</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/89369.The_Te_of_Piglet" title="Paperback 9781405204279" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1138604373">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3604687459005104675">After enjoying Benjamin Hoff's Tao of Pooh, with its delightful introduction of Taoist ideas using the classic A.A. Milne characters, I approached the rather thicker Te of Piglet with gleeful anticipation. Alas, if only the book were thinner.<br /><br />There are still the interactions, albeit somewhat less adorable, with the denizens of the Hundred-Acre Wood, but they are nearly suffocated by lengthy broadsides against all sorts of political targets, from anti-Environmental Business to Technology, from Sci</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3604687459005104675">After enjoying Benjamin Hoff's Tao of Pooh, with its delightful introduction of Taoist ideas using the classic A.A. Milne characters, I approached the rather thicker Te of Piglet with gleeful anticipation. Alas, if only the book were thinner.<br /><br />There are still the interactions, albeit somewhat less adorable, with the denizens of the Hundred-Acre Wood, but they are nearly suffocated by lengthy broadsides against all sorts of political targets, from anti-Environmental Business to Technology, from Scientists to Western Medicine. And while I am not at all opposed to the notion of screeding to one's heart's content, the avalanche of Haughty Thoughts seems entirely out of place given both the first book and the greater Poohniverse.<br /><br />Of particular oddity was his railing against Grammar "Amazons" whose great crime against humanity -- suggesting "he or she" or "they/them" constructions rather than "he" as the default -- apparently results in the utter emasculation of Men and the Death of Civilization. For a school of thought like Taoism that has had some sort of inclusiveness for women in it from the beginning (centuries, now), it seems a really weird hill to die on.<br /><br />I'll confess, I nearly put the book down at that point. I was reminded of Dorothy Parker (as Constant Reader) reviewing The House at Pooh Corner -- "And it is that word 'hummy,' my darlings, that marks the first place in The House at Pooh Corner at which Tonstant Weader fwowed up." What the hell, Hoff?<br /><br />I had a brief urge to edit an expurgated version of the Te of Piglet with the politics dialed back several notches (and a hundred pages or so) so that the lovely Taoist concepts can shine forth. But then I remembered this is but one tiny rock in the stream of Taoist literature and, in the spirit of such things, I should be like water and simply route around it.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3604687459005104675">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer121460521">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9522067788173069205">This is like night and day compared to the Tao of Pooh.<br /><br />Tao of Pooh alternated between scenes from Pooh and Hoff's explanation of what that meant in Taoist terms. The Pooh scenes really helped illustrate what he was discussing. The Te of Piglet consists more of exposition about the concept of Te and Taoism in general. There's very little effort made to tie it back to Piglet and the other Pooh characters and quite a bit of political ranting.<br /><br />The few points he tried to make about Piglet he'd already</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9522067788173069205">This is like night and day compared to the Tao of Pooh.<br /><br />Tao of Pooh alternated between scenes from Pooh and Hoff's explanation of what that meant in Taoist terms. The Pooh scenes really helped illustrate what he was discussing. The Te of Piglet consists more of exposition about the concept of Te and Taoism in general. There's very little effort made to tie it back to Piglet and the other Pooh characters and quite a bit of political ranting.<br /><br />The few points he tried to make about Piglet he'd already made in the Tao of Pooh - complete with the same examples from the same stories. There's very little Piglet and an abundance of Hoff's own opinions on how Taoism/Buddhism relate to modern society. He discusses everything from feminism being horrible, to smoking, to farming and fertilization practices, to the evils of radiation and therefore modern electronics.<br /><br />While I agree with some of the points he made, I didn't pick up the book to read how he's applied Taoism to his political philosophy and world view. <br /><br />In short - just stop with the Tao of Pooh. It covers the main points of Te as well.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8720308045256148760">The book is supposed to demonstrate how Piglet in the original A.A. Milne writings personified Te, or 'Virtue in Action'. <br /><br />To illustrate this the author wrote a bunch of new situations for Piglet. Which defeats the entire purpose, since it forces Piglet into the mold for the philosophy instead of showing how he already conforms to it.<br /><br />The author also spends a lot of time explaining how the world is in poor shape due to the "Eeyores" who complain about how the world is doomed instead of seeing thin</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8720308045256148760">The book is supposed to demonstrate how Piglet in the original A.A. Milne writings personified Te, or 'Virtue in Action'. <br /><br />To illustrate this the author wrote a bunch of new situations for Piglet. Which defeats the entire purpose, since it forces Piglet into the mold for the philosophy instead of showing how he already conforms to it.<br /><br />The author also spends a lot of time explaining how the world is in poor shape due to the "Eeyores" who complain about how the world is doomed instead of seeing things as they really are.<br />He then writes a final chapter explaining how the world is doomed, and everything is going to fall apart, and Western Civilization sucks and there's no hope for anything and if only people had listened to Tao and Te things would have turned out much better.<br /><br />Even I who agree (mostly), with his political and philosophical viewpoints, couldn't help feeling the book was self-righteous and superior-than-thou. He makes a few decent points but he defends them with poor logic and with 'examples' he made up himself. </span>

  <div class="xhr_comment_photo">
    	<a href="/user/show/9031378-taylor-g" alt="Taylor G."><img alt="9031378" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1335313190p1/9031378.jpg" /></a>

    <a href="/user/show/9031378-taylor-g" style="font-weight:bold">Taylor G.</a>

      
<span id="freeTextContainer11580776194008334469">Hi Danny, I have been reading The Te of Piglet over the past weeks and this book has left me with some of the ideas you have. I didn't read his first</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11580776194008334469">Hi Danny, I have been reading The Te of Piglet over the past weeks and this book has left me with some of the ideas you have. I didn't read his first book, The Tao of Pooh so it was a little hard to keep up with this book but it helped me understand Taoism more. The book does focus on Piglet more than any other character. Taoist try to reach the state of Perfect Virtue through discarding whatever prevents harmony with Tao. They also believe that Piglet is the best animal suited to demonstrate the transformation of Virtue. The Te in the Te of Piglet stands for a quality in a special type of character; an individual that has spiritual strength or hidden potential. In this book they use Piglet as this individual because he possesses potential and may not even be aware of it sometimes.<br />I do agree with Hoff when he says that the “Eeyores” of the world are not making the world any better. Eeyore always thinks about the negative things that can happen in life. He doesn’t only bring sadness in his life but also in Piglet’s life. In the book Eeyore says, “Be careful, little Piglet-someone might pick you along with those pansies and put you in a vase on the mantelpiece. And then what would you do?” Eeyore is always trying to bring Piglet down by calling him small and bring his self-esteem down. That’s what the people in today’s world are doing and it’s not making anything better. <br />I also don’t think that Hoff is trying to say Westernization sucks and is the reason why the world is falling apart. I think he is trying to say that Tigger resembles the “western ways” because he is restless and can’t control himself. The “Tiggers” of the world think they are larger than life and bounce everywhere and over anything.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11580776194008334469">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer15397319615990524215"> At first I enjoyed it for what I was learning about Chinese philosophy.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText15397319615990524215">	At first I enjoyed it for what I was learning about Chinese philosophy. But then he gets very political about current events in 1992, which, while also being outdated now, are not as relevant to the timeless themes he is discussing as he would think. He gets on his soapbox about his personal views, which I also see as off target and an annoyance. I wanted to read about Te and it’s relationship to Piglet, not on how he feels all the past presidents have overlooked environmental protection. Actually, if he simply mentioned that it wouldn’t be so bad. But he gets on such a strong winded tangent with the details that no one, over 10 years later, are going to be impressed by what he is referencing. 									 <br /><br />He has made this book outdated and so I would not necessarily recommend it to anyone.     <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_392770720">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/392770720" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5715723-krista" class="left imgcol" title="Krista"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1310698282p2/5715723.jpg" alt="Krista" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/392770720?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 15, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5715723-krista" class="user" title="Krista">Krista</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/89369.The_Te_of_Piglet" title="Paperback 9781405204279" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5715723-krista?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5715723-krista?shelf=spirituality" class="actionLinkLite">spirituality</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer392770720">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14169915268059433697">I wanted to like this book. I remember enjoying "The Tao of Pooh" and I naturally figured this would be a companion piece in the same vein. However, in the ten years that passed between that publication and this one, Benjamin Hoff seems to have grown into a grouchy, cantankerous old man decrying the youth of America and using this book as a soapbox for his own political rants that have naught to do with Taoism, Te, Piglet or Pooh.<br /><br />The book starts off fine, with some basic introductions of Taoism</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14169915268059433697">I wanted to like this book. I remember enjoying "The Tao of Pooh" and I naturally figured this would be a companion piece in the same vein. However, in the ten years that passed between that publication and this one, Benjamin Hoff seems to have grown into a grouchy, cantankerous old man decrying the youth of America and using this book as a soapbox for his own political rants that have naught to do with Taoism, Te, Piglet or Pooh.<br /><br />The book starts off fine, with some basic introductions of Taoism and Te, but things start to go downhill with the chapter titled "The Eeyore Effect." Midway through this chapter, Hoff suddenly starts on his own personal tangents, ranting about everyone from the news media to modern educators to so-called "Amazon Feminists" intent on converting all nouns to gender-neutral descriptions because they are both "antimasculine" and "overmasculine" and have "no respect for the feminine."<br /><br />Don't ask me to make sense of that. It's WTF bizarre in the context of the book. It's quite obvious that Hoff despised feminists and political correctness (which was just coming into vogue when this book was written) and decided to use this book as his soapbox.<br /><br />Then there's the ranting about "kids these days." I'm 30 now, so I'm part of the youth generation he was ranting about in the early 90's when this was written. According to Hoff, it was horrible that my generation was being taught to use computers in school. And of course he blames low test scores on video games:<br /><br />"The minds of American children are now so stressed and crippled by Tiggerish wham-bam Video Games, Television Shows, and Instant Left-Brain Computer Activities that many of them are unable to concentrate on anything for more than five minutes."<br /><br />and<br /><br />"Perhaps today's children will be able to find employment years from now, anyway -- they may be hired as technical laborers by the Chinese, Japanese, and Koreans, who will by then own just about everything because their cultures far more than ours encourage children to Focus on the Mind."<br /><br />Ho hum. They same thing people have been complaining about for generations. Kids these days, no respect, no proper education. Okay, grouchy old man. Sounds like my grandfather, ranting about how China and "the Japs" were taking over the country.<br /><br />At this point I stopped reading the book in depth and just skimmed the rest. It's short enough that I didn't miss much. Yet the bizarre paranoid rants just get worse:<br /><br />"Today the public is being captivated by computers, word processors, and the like -- whose cathode-ray tubes emit X-ray radiation, and whose circuits and display terminals produce strong electromagnetic fields. {...} The latest Popular Radiation device is the Microwave Oven, which bombards food with high-frequency electromagnetic radiation, irritating it until it heats up. This sort of Perversion of Nature is perfectly safe, authorities insist -- if it weren't, the Wonderful Machines wouldn't be on the market. Maybe the authorities are correct this time, just for once. On the other hand, maybe they're not."<br /><br />In addition to being afraid of microwaved food and feminists, Hoff also has nothing nice to say about Conservatives, turning a chapter ironically titled "The Day of Piglet" into a political soapbox rant about self-righteous religious conservative politicians who keep money for themselves while widows and orphans starve in the streets. This segues into a sort of environmental hippie treatise about the billions of dollars the US was spending on the first Iraq war for Kuwait's oil supplies while "across the country untaxed gang lords cruise about in limousines, drug pushers and psychopaths prey on neglected children, homeless grandmothers push their worldly possessions in shopping carts, citizens of all ages contract Pistol Fever, shooting themselves and each other..." Then there's all the toxic waste, pesticides, and chemicals in the environment that are slowly killing us...<br /><br />Um.<br /><br />Piglet...? Pooh...? Taoism? Te?<br /><br />It's amazing how this short little book manages to go so completely off topic so quickly. Also, I've never read a book about Eastern thought that was so negative and borderline misanthropic.<br /><br />"The Tao of Pooh" is worth a read, but do yourself a favor and don't bother with this one.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14169915268059433697">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_343251011">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/343251011" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7663266-corbitt" class="left imgcol" title="Corbitt"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1345401659p2/7663266.jpg" alt="Corbitt" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/343251011?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 05, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7663266-corbitt" class="user" title="Corbitt">Corbitt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/89369.The_Te_of_Piglet" title="Paperback 9781405204279" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer343251011">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4268872141295687528">This book is about 100 pages, give or take, larger than the first book on the subject matter by Hoff, The Tao of Pooh. While explaining the principles of Taoism through stories from A.A. Milne and crafting new dialogue for the characters still works, it is far less a part of this book than the previous. In fact, this book tends to go off into explanation far more than crafting examples using the character.<br /><br />But the single unforgivable part of this book is the large amount of soap box talk the auth</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4268872141295687528">This book is about 100 pages, give or take, larger than the first book on the subject matter by Hoff, The Tao of Pooh. While explaining the principles of Taoism through stories from A.A. Milne and crafting new dialogue for the characters still works, it is far less a part of this book than the previous. In fact, this book tends to go off into explanation far more than crafting examples using the character.<br /><br />But the single unforgivable part of this book is the large amount of soap box talk the author throws in to the book. He has a clear stance on government and social issues going on at the time of his writing the book and he uses points between talking over principles to...well rage on about them.<br /><br />Oh, and he basically claims a doomsday is coming.<br /><br />The Tao of Pooh was a delightful introduction into the tenets of Taoism using timeless characters. This book takes a stab at doing it again with a little expansion, succeeds at points but mostly preaches to you. I have no problem with him sharing his views or believing that we have some kind of great transition coming in this world...but this was not the forum for it. Poorly played, author.<br /><br />I highly recommend the Tao of Pooh. I only recommend this companion if you like being made uncomfortable and angry towards the ends of books.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4268872141295687528">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/545729056?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 25, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2475612-kyle-muntz" class="user" title="Kyle Muntz">Kyle Muntz</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer545729056">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3256495423955249002">Read this real quick for fun since someone I know sent me a copy for free. It's silly, kind of retarded, and didactic in a way that sort of reminds me of elementary school, but at the same time I couldn't help being impressed by Taoism as a premodern system of thought, even when parts of the book seemed to be made up more of Toff's opinions than Taoism itself. As a philosophy, Taoism seems a little quaint and insubstantial, but it makes up for it by being genuinely appreciative of life, gentle,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3256495423955249002">Read this real quick for fun since someone I know sent me a copy for free. It's silly, kind of retarded, and didactic in a way that sort of reminds me of elementary school, but at the same time I couldn't help being impressed by Taoism as a premodern system of thought, even when parts of the book seemed to be made up more of Toff's opinions than Taoism itself. As a philosophy, Taoism seems a little quaint and insubstantial, but it makes up for it by being genuinely appreciative of life, gentle, and not gendered. In these areas at least, it's an improvement on early Western philosophy (which, despite being a step forward in so many ways and much more intellectually rigorous, was infected with the same arrogance, authoritarianism, and male-dominance that basically characterizes the history of the western world). I can't claim to have a broad knowledge of eastern ideas, but the Tao seems to fit better alongside Bhuddism and gets away from the ritualized hierarchies of Confucius. (Not even going to compare it to western religions.) <br /><br />Plus this book is kind of cute. And the pictures were nice. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3256495423955249002">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_54542704">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/54542704" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2214498-purplycookie" class="left imgcol" title="PurplyCookie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1415491715p2/2214498.jpg" alt="PurplyCookie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/54542704?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 08, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2214498-purplycookie" class="user" title="PurplyCookie">PurplyCookie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/89369.The_Te_of_Piglet" title="Paperback 9781405204279" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2214498-purplycookie?shelf=religious" class="actionLinkLite">religious</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2214498-purplycookie?shelf=philosophy" class="actionLinkLite">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer54542704">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10421665198812836492">I loved <strong>"The Tao of Pooh"</strong> since I felt like I was being taught Taoist philosophy from a new perspective. A refreshing perspective that I have never felt when studying Chinese Philosophy in college. <br /><br />That's what I naturally thought that I was getting into with the <strong>"The Te of Piglet."</strong> But I was quite disappointed to find out that there's much more ranting than philosophy in this book. Hoff flirts with the idea briefly, but instead uses Piglet as a soap box to attack the Eeyores of the world. But wh</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10421665198812836492">I loved <strong>"The Tao of Pooh"</strong> since I felt like I was being taught Taoist philosophy from a new perspective. A refreshing perspective that I have never felt when studying Chinese Philosophy in college. <br /><br />That's what I naturally thought that I was getting into with the <strong>"The Te of Piglet."</strong> But I was quite disappointed to find out that there's much more ranting than philosophy in this book. Hoff flirts with the idea briefly, but instead uses Piglet as a soap box to attack the Eeyores of the world. But why Piglet as the embodiment of the Virtue Te? <em>In many ways, Piglet may appear the least significant of the Pooh characters. Yet he is the only one of them to change, to grow, to become more than what he was in the first place. And in the end, he does this not by denying his smallness, but by applying it, for the good of others.</em><br /><br />The one standout out of the entire book was the chapter on "The Eeyore Effect". Especially hilarious and illuminating at the same time was the story about 'The Critics' wherein a Hindu, a Rabbi, and a Critic were caught in a thunderstorm. I felt that the chapter "The Tigger Tendency" could have been explored in a less scathing way in connection to our modern-day tendencies and attitudes. <br /><br />It went downhill from there, what with Hoff's long winded exploration of "Things As They Might Be" which only served as a soapbox for all that he thinks is wrong in the world and in society today. The only memorable part of it was the Chinese Story of "The Well by the Road" which examines how one's perception of given situations are altered based on where you're coming from or what side of the fence you happen to be on. <br /><br /><em>Maybe the "good" things are tests, possibly difficult ones at that, and the "bad" things are gifts to help us grow: problems to solve, situations to learn how to avoid, habits to change, conditions to accept, lessons to learn, things to transform--all opportunities to find Wisdom, Happiness, and Truth. </em><br /><br />To fill the book up, the author seems to wind himself into a twisted rage, berating everything in the world which is not him, for being shallow, self-obsessed, and destructive. In the end, what you get is a lengthy rant about the authors' attacks towards Confucianism Christianity, Feminism, Republicans, critics, computers, scattered with a few quotes from the <strong>"Winnie the Pooh"</strong> books as mere dressing.<br /><br /><br /><u>Book Details: </u><br /><br /><u>Title</u> The Te of Piglet<br /><u>Author</u> Benjamin Hoff <br /><u>Reviewed By</u> Purplycookie</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10421665198812836492">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/578544424?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 02, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5471426-emma" class="user" title="Emma">Emma</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/933398.The_Te_Of_Piglet" title=" 9780749315146" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer578544424">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6254967336941078652">In all honesty, this book was extremely disappointing compared to the first. Instead of focusing on Taoist beliefs and how the Pooh characters exhibit them, Benjamin spent much more time talking about politics, and how our country is being run wrong. Which I normally wouldn't have minded too much, because as it turns out, Mr. Hoff and I are more or less on the same page politically, but at times you could just feel his anger at the state of the country (And considering that at this point this bo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6254967336941078652">In all honesty, this book was extremely disappointing compared to the first. Instead of focusing on Taoist beliefs and how the Pooh characters exhibit them, Benjamin spent much more time talking about politics, and how our country is being run wrong. Which I normally wouldn't have minded too much, because as it turns out, Mr. Hoff and I are more or less on the same page politically, but at times you could just feel his anger at the state of the country (And considering that at this point this book is 20 years old and we still face many of the same problems, I'm not going to try to tell you that our country is in a good place yet.) And yet while I agreed with a lot of what he was saying, it felt like he was yelling at me, or trying to argue with me, while I was on the same page as him. <br /><br />I also had a bit of a problem with the way he treated the Pooh characters in this book. He was kind of mean and condescending to Owl, Eeyore, and Rabbit, and spoke to Pooh like he was an idiot. (I'm not trying to argue that Pooh isn't simple minded, it's just that he's endearingly so, and reading someone mocking him is off-putting) He also changed Piglet's essence in order to suit his purposes. <br /><br />I can't tell you that it wasn't a good book, because it wasn't horrid, but it just wasn't a very good book on Taoism.</span>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_119819201">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/119819201" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1140398-marcy" class="left imgcol" title="Marcy"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1209997030p2/1140398.jpg" alt="Marcy" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/119819201?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 02, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1140398-marcy" class="user" title="Marcy">Marcy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/89369.The_Te_of_Piglet" title="Paperback 9781405204279" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer119819201">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1746705446656711270">Benjamin Hoff is much stronger in his own personal views of how people in the U.S. need to behave in order to follow a better Taoist life. He criticizes so much that is wrong - toxic waste, inhumane treatment of others, the amount of money that has gone to the military, etc. He often goes in tirades, taking away my appreciation for the more gentle style and powerful wisdom in which he wrote the Tao of Pooh. <br /><br />I did appreciate Hoff's characterization of Piglet, the character in Winnie the Pooh who</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1746705446656711270">Benjamin Hoff is much stronger in his own personal views of how people in the U.S. need to behave in order to follow a better Taoist life. He criticizes so much that is wrong - toxic waste, inhumane treatment of others, the amount of money that has gone to the military, etc. He often goes in tirades, taking away my appreciation for the more gentle style and powerful wisdom in which he wrote the Tao of Pooh. <br /><br />I did appreciate Hoff's characterization of Piglet, the character in Winnie the Pooh who is always fearful, yet changes when he develops the courage to escape for help when Pooh and Owl are trapped in Owl's house after it had crashed to the ground. Piglet gained confidence and developed a "growth" mindset, rather than the "fixed" mindset he had had. <br /><br />Hoff's challenge is for people to change for the better in order to be more compassionate and change the evils of the world before it is destroyed by humans. Piglet is his example of "transformation," a hope for the world's future.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1746705446656711270">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/563865207?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 18, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9337058-p-w" class="user" title="P.W.">P.W.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/89369.The_Te_of_Piglet" title="Paperback 9781405204279" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/9337058-p-w?shelf=warren-s-one-stars" class="actionLinkLite">warren-s-one-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer563865207">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10409992599502134017">This book was originally rated at two stars by me because it really paled in comparison to the Tao of Pooh, which I still highly recommend.<br /><br />Why is this book one star? A few reasons....<br /><br />1. Hoff clearly didn't want to write it, from the way he was describing in the beginning. I don't know if there was a contractual obligation or he just realized that he liked money, but he already went into it with a little less than "pure" intent.<br /><br />2. While there was still some charm in the book, it wasn't as good wi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10409992599502134017">This book was originally rated at two stars by me because it really paled in comparison to the Tao of Pooh, which I still highly recommend.<br /><br />Why is this book one star? A few reasons....<br /><br />1. Hoff clearly didn't want to write it, from the way he was describing in the beginning. I don't know if there was a contractual obligation or he just realized that he liked money, but he already went into it with a little less than "pure" intent.<br /><br />2. While there was still some charm in the book, it wasn't as good with tying philosophies into the other animals in the Hundred-Acre wood. A good attempt is made with Piglet, and even some with Tigger, but Rabbit and Owl just kind of fall flat.<br /><br />3. Did you ever read "The Jungle" by Upton Sinclair? It's a great book till the end, where it becomes insufferably preachy. This book veers into similar territory with a completely irrelevant bashfest on American political conservatives, while remaining <strong>completely silent</strong> on problems that may exist with the other side of the political aisle. <br /><br />He even goes so far to bash Gulf War I while saying that we could learn a lot from Chinese philosophy.<br /><br />Here's the issue with that... Gulf War I was in about 1991-2.... China had the Tienanmen Square Massacre in 1989. If you want to contrast ancient philosophy with modern society but don't critically analyze all sides you are doing yourself a disservice.<br /><br /><strong>I would recommend this book for:</strong> People who have it all figured out already and know that they are never ever wrong.<br /><br /><br /><strong>I would <u>not</u> recommend this book for:</strong> True Pooh fans, and people who found the Tao of Pooh an endearing comparison of classic literature to classic philosophy.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10409992599502134017">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/42978196?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 31, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1041790-chris" class="user" title="Chris">Chris</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <a href="/book/show/933398.The_Te_Of_Piglet" title=" 9780749315146" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer42978196">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7790462503308911009">After reading this book, I cannot look back at <em>The Tao of Pooh</em> without thinking of Pooh an unthinking dimwit. The author, Hoff, tries to explain living with virtue and in harmony with the natural world or <em> Te<em></em>. Hoff, does a good job of using previously written works to illustrate his points, but comes short when using Milne's characters. In fact as the book went on, I began to dislike the character of little Piglet.<br />My major gripe with this book is that Ben Hoff decided to pull out his soapbox and</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7790462503308911009">After reading this book, I cannot look back at <em>The Tao of Pooh</em> without thinking of Pooh an unthinking dimwit. The author, Hoff, tries to explain living with virtue and in harmony with the natural world or <em> Te<em></em>. Hoff, does a good job of using previously written works to illustrate his points, but comes short when using Milne's characters. In fact as the book went on, I began to dislike the character of little Piglet.<br />My major gripe with this book is that Ben Hoff decided to pull out his soapbox and get all spill his personal politics all over his book's information. I ended up skipping pages to get past Hoff's tirades on how we should live in sod houses eating only the twigs and berries that grow on our front lawns, and how all the world's problems would be solved by sitting in a drum circle or hunger strikes, hoping the rest of the world notices, a la Gandhi.<br />I can't be receptive to "do good things and good things will happen to you" and The Golden Rule, when I'm subjected to a hysterical ranting on how ashamed I should be for eating animal based protein in the same pararagraph.<br /></em></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_85361828">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/85361828" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/515191-matthew-rasnake" class="left imgcol" title="Matthew Rasnake"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1323794014p2/515191.jpg" alt="Matthew Rasnake" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/85361828?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 13, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/515191-matthew-rasnake" class="user" title="Matthew Rasnake">Matthew Rasnake</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/89369.The_Te_of_Piglet" title="Paperback 9781405204279" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">nobody</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/515191-matthew-rasnake?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/515191-matthew-rasnake?shelf=philosophy" class="actionLinkLite">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer85361828">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14912229668421098621">At the beginning of this book, the author mentions how he hadn't intended to write a sequel to his original "The Tao of Pooh," but since that book had been such a Remarkable Success, he eventually just had to. After reading this book, it seems to me that it would have been better if he hadn't. Perhaps he was short of ideas, or hurting for money, or trying to recapture earlier success, but whatever the reason it seems he produced something that didn't really even captivate himself.<br /><br />Honestly, it's</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14912229668421098621">At the beginning of this book, the author mentions how he hadn't intended to write a sequel to his original "The Tao of Pooh," but since that book had been such a Remarkable Success, he eventually just had to. After reading this book, it seems to me that it would have been better if he hadn't. Perhaps he was short of ideas, or hurting for money, or trying to recapture earlier success, but whatever the reason it seems he produced something that didn't really even captivate himself.<br /><br />Honestly, it's been a while since I've read "The Tao of Pooh," and it's possible there was just as much political posturing as is found in this book, but I don't recall it that way. My memory says "The Tao of Pooh" was a well constructed, reasonably presented book with a coherent message. My memory says "The Tao of Pooh" achieved its goal in illustrating Taoist principles using excerpts and examples from Pooh stories.<br /><br />Unfortunately, "The Te of Piglet" doesn't live up to its pedigree. It's a disjointed diatribe hiding behind an alluring premise. There's quite a bit of railing against modern society and its evils, and quite a bit about how the Eeyores, Rabbits, and Owls of the world are causing so much disharmony and destruction. There's even a bit about how Piglet is a Very Small Animal with a Very Big Heart, which seemingly is the purpose of the book, but which gets bulldozed over by the author's pre-occupation with pushing his own political agenda. And I'm saying this as someone who generally agrees with the author's political agenda.<br /><br />Ultimately, I didn't buy this book to get assaulted by politics, I read it to get a unique perspective on an Eastern philosophy that I admire. And that, sadly, seems to have only been the secondary or tertiary purpose of this book for the author.<br /><br />If you liked "The Tao of Pooh," do yourself a favor and don't pick this up. In some ways, I feel this book has ruined my perception of the earlier book, and of the author himself, which is a shame.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14912229668421098621">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer42234098">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18405943728203117708">I shyed away from these books (The Tao of Pooh and The Te of Piglet) for so long because of their popularity, and I never was much of a Winnie the Pooh fan. I decided to read this book after a dream I had last year while commuting between Bend and Portland. An Asian man named "Te Walker" was leading a group of women through a treacherous mountain pass. I had the dream the night before I was to drive through the snowy pass around Mt. Hood, by scene of the accident I'd had the year before. I resea</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18405943728203117708">I shyed away from these books (The Tao of Pooh and The Te of Piglet) for so long because of their popularity, and I never was much of a Winnie the Pooh fan. I decided to read this book after a dream I had last year while commuting between Bend and Portland. An Asian man named "Te Walker" was leading a group of women through a treacherous mountain pass. I had the dream the night before I was to drive through the snowy pass around Mt. Hood, by scene of the accident I'd had the year before. I researched the word "te", which in Chinese means "virtue" and "power", and decided to read the Te of Piglet, in addition to the Tao te Ching, etc. Thus began my journey into exploring Taoism (after a brief stint in my early 20's), and I am thankful for the dream and glad I read the Te of Piglet. An amusing, depthful read with some political/social/cultural commentaray, using the meek figure of Piglet as Taoist symbol. (January 26th is Confucian/Buddhist/Taoist New Year).</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18405943728203117708">...more</a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <a href="/user/show/1394221-adam" class="user" title="Adam">Adam</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <a href="/book/show/89369.The_Te_of_Piglet" title="Paperback 9781405204279" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1394221-adam?shelf=philosophy" class="actionLinkLite">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer29199851">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2607995830960807822">This book purports to elucidate the meaning of Te (virtue/power) via an analysis of the Winnie-the-Pooh stories. But it’s mostly just Hoff complaining about modern life, with little apparent structure or unifying theme. And he’s not very good at it: a mediocre ranter at best. Allow me to quote some of his half-assed complaints*: “Modern people bloat life with pointless complications.” “Feminists make words too complicated.” “People run on paved paths rather than on natural grass.” Meh. He also c</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2607995830960807822">This book purports to elucidate the meaning of Te (virtue/power) via an analysis of the Winnie-the-Pooh stories. But it’s mostly just Hoff complaining about modern life, with little apparent structure or unifying theme. And he’s not very good at it: a mediocre ranter at best. Allow me to quote some of his half-assed complaints*: “Modern people bloat life with pointless complications.” “Feminists make words too complicated.” “People run on paved paths rather than on natural grass.” Meh. He also casts most of Milne’s characters, such as Tigger, Owl, and Eeyore, as villains, dismissing the breadth of sympathy in the original stories. It’s all very overbearing in its scattershot bitterness. And usually I can’t get enough bitterness.<br /><br />According to Wikipedia, “In 2006, Hoff published an essay online denouncing the publishing industry and announcing his resignation from book-writing.” That sounds about right.<br /><br />*not exact quotes.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1064826592">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3483945033618839493">I think Hoff has some interesting and relevant points. The world does need to focus on the typically feminine--the ability to accept things without reacting against them, the ability to think situations to their conclusion, the ability to search for peace instead of jumping to war. However, he does seem to idolize China, which bothered me since currently China is ruled by a Dictatorship who most definitely is not Taoist. <br />Although I always have trouble with people who present the good of one peop</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3483945033618839493">I think Hoff has some interesting and relevant points. The world does need to focus on the typically feminine--the ability to accept things without reacting against them, the ability to think situations to their conclusion, the ability to search for peace instead of jumping to war. However, he does seem to idolize China, which bothered me since currently China is ruled by a Dictatorship who most definitely is not Taoist. <br />Although I always have trouble with people who present the good of one people/thing (China) and contrast it with the bad, and only the bad, of another people/thing (America).<br /><br />I did enjoy the read, and I found it interesting just how similar this book is to More's <em>Utopia</em> and Alexander Boganov's <em>Red Star</em>, both presentations of an ideal society. Clearly, people all over the world have similar ideas about the perfect world.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1386461578?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 09, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6643518-jenny-riley" class="user" title="Jenny Riley">Jenny Riley</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/89369.The_Te_of_Piglet" title="Paperback 9781405204279" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1386461578">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8956999874742348485">Really not up to the standard of The Tao of Pooh at all - this book was clearly written as a sequel for the sake of a sequel, and not because the author necessarily had more to say on the subject (which, given the subject matter and the excellence of the first book, was a real let down). He even admits as much pretty much in the first chapter!<br />It's unclear to me (having read this twice now) what the message was fundamentally in the book, apart from complaining that everyone seems to be 'doing it</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8956999874742348485">Really not up to the standard of The Tao of Pooh at all - this book was clearly written as a sequel for the sake of a sequel, and not because the author necessarily had more to say on the subject (which, given the subject matter and the excellence of the first book, was a real let down). He even admits as much pretty much in the first chapter!<br />It's unclear to me (having read this twice now) what the message was fundamentally in the book, apart from complaining that everyone seems to be 'doing it wrong'. Winnie the Pooh stories were not used nearly as effectively to make points, seemingly being forced into a point that wasn't there, and although the conversations with the characters were sometimes charming and funny, they felt stilted this time. Also, personally, I disagree with the author's views on feminism, but I wonder if he wrote this again today he might alter the views slightly (he still views most feminists as aggressive bra burners, and I hope as a society we are now past this view of the term 'feminism' and 'feminist'). <br />Overall a truly disappointing sequel. I still whole heartedly recommend The Tao of Pooh, but would suggest you leave this one on the shelf - sorry to be so brutal about what could have been a splendid book. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8956999874742348485">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1299627360">

  <a href="/user/show/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all" class="left imgcol" title="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1406361683p2/20738920.jpg" alt="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" /></a>

      <a href="/user/show/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all" class="user" title="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)">Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/89369.The_Te_of_Piglet" title="Paperback 9781405204279" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=ripped-off-original-source" class="actionLinkLite">ripped-off-original-source</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1299627360">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16893749951411346699">I would like to know how Hoff got permission to commandeer all the Ernest K. Shepherd illustrations, and use Milne's characters for a book that has really nothing to do with the work of either artist. Obviously he must have paid for the privilege; but why was his money accepted? Perhaps because the heirs of Shepherd and Milne no longer care <em>(there is, after all, the whole Disney thing)</em> or because it's no longer about the people, but the corporate interests. <em>(There is, after all, the whole Disney</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16893749951411346699">I would like to know how Hoff got permission to commandeer all the Ernest K. Shepherd illustrations, and use Milne's characters for a book that has really nothing to do with the work of either artist. Obviously he must have paid for the privilege; but why was his money accepted? Perhaps because the heirs of Shepherd and Milne no longer care <em>(there is, after all, the whole Disney thing)</em> or because it's no longer about the people, but the corporate interests. <em>(There is, after all, the whole Disney thing).</em> Maybe the rights don't belong to a real person anymore.<br /><br />In any case, this book is enormously self-serving, self-centred and self-indulgent. I liked the first bit, but the further I got into it, the more I found myself thinking "Wait--what?" Rather like Piglet when he was running through the wood with his balloon and felldowngoBOOM. By the time Hoff got to the purported deleterious effects of microwaves on our food and computer screens on our bodies, I was shaking my head. He doesn't seem to have internalised anything he himself says (at great length) about the Critics who Set Themselves Up as Judge and Jury on All Things. Um, yeah--just like Hoff. <br /><br />The Seventies became the Nineties. Which have nearly become the 2020s. Unlike the base texts which Hoff purports to base this 250 page rant on (Chinese as well as British), The RanTe of Hoff will not be an enduring classic. It doesn't even bear re-reading. <br /><br />If you want to learn about Piglet's worldview, you can't do better than to read him as Milne wrote him. We who have known and loved him since we were very young don't need the filter of someone else's pseudophilosophical ruminations <em>(ie, things that make you throw up in your mouth a little)</em> to appreciate him as he really is. The same might be said for Taoist thought. Not that I'm an expert by any means, but one wonders how much Hoff actually knows about Chinese people and how they really see the world. He seems to idealise and romanticise <em>his</em> idea of "their" thought to an inordinate degree, without really being aware of what's going on on that side of the world.<br /><br />I wonder what the Chinese think of him. <em>Oh, right--they don't know he exists.</em><br /><br />Hardly surprising.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16893749951411346699">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1102730415">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5934349359411151011">This book purported to be a lighthearted discussion of Taoism using Pooh characters (especially humble little Piglet) as examples. There were some gems here and there, especially in the traditional Eastern stories that the author told. But to get to the gems I had to wade through the author's political diatribes, his anti-feminist rants, his criticisms of national and world policies, etc. I assume the author was trying to show us how apply philosophy to daily life, which is great. But this autho</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5934349359411151011">This book purported to be a lighthearted discussion of Taoism using Pooh characters (especially humble little Piglet) as examples. There were some gems here and there, especially in the traditional Eastern stories that the author told. But to get to the gems I had to wade through the author's political diatribes, his anti-feminist rants, his criticisms of national and world policies, etc. I assume the author was trying to show us how apply philosophy to daily life, which is great. But this author's personal philosophy seemed to be to hate everyone who didn't agree with him and to write a book criticizing their ideologies. And perhaps I'm wrong, but this didn't really seem to be in line with the Taoist philosophies the book was trying to present.<br /><br />I haven't read <a title="The Tao of Pooh by Benjamin Hoff" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48757.The_Tao_of_Pooh">The Tao of Pooh</a> and it looks like other reviewers consider that book to be much better than this one. Maybe I'll give it a try if I run across it. But I thought this one was a waste of time considering all the other better books out there.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1067313009">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1067313009" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/20385261-ray-jackson" class="left imgcol" title="Ray Jackson"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1437154863p2/20385261.jpg" alt="Ray Jackson" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1067313009?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 29, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/20385261-ray-jackson" class="user" title="Ray Jackson">Ray Jackson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/89369.The_Te_of_Piglet" title="Paperback 9781405204279" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1067313009">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12252263068955052298">This book has often be called "an excellent introduction to Taoism". And that's exactly what it is, if Taoism means judging, being intolerant, telling which things and which faiths are good and which are bad, and looking down on everything and everyone who is different. Somehow, I think Taoism is something else... <br /><br />I gave this book 4 stars. Two for being another reason to re-read "Winnie-the-Pooh" and two for a few popular quotations of Eastern thinkers. I would have added an another half-star fo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12252263068955052298">This book has often be called "an excellent introduction to Taoism". And that's exactly what it is, if Taoism means judging, being intolerant, telling which things and which faiths are good and which are bad, and looking down on everything and everyone who is different. Somehow, I think Taoism is something else... <br /><br />I gave this book 4 stars. Two for being another reason to re-read "Winnie-the-Pooh" and two for a few popular quotations of Eastern thinkers. I would have added an another half-star for the several nice insights the author didn't quite manage to spoil if not for the author's annoying habit of trying to write in the simple, yet brilliant style of Milne and failing miserably. <br /><br />Benjamin Hoff writes about what happens when you try to fit round pegs in square holes, yet this is exactly what HE does. He doesn't accept Eeyore, Piglet, Rabbit, Tigger and Owl as they are, instead he describes Owl as "the dried-up Western descendant of the Confucianist Dedicated Scholar" (not the only place in the book where he bashes Confucius and his followers) and "one who studies Knowledge for the sake of Knowledge, and who keeps what he learns to himself or to his own small group, writing pompous and pretentious papers that no one else can understand, rather than working for the Enlightenment of others". Pure I-know-better criticism. <br /><br />Therefore, instead of reading about "Nearsighted Science" etc., just go and re-read "Winnie-the-Pooh". You'll understand Taoism a hundred times better by doing that (or doing nothing at all) than by reading "The Tao Of Pooh". <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12252263068955052298">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
